    Discovery of a Small-Molecule Inhibitor of Interleukin 15: Pharmacophore-Based Virtual Screening and Hit Optimization

    Abstract

    Interleukin (IL)-15 is a pleiotropic cytokine, which is structurally close to IL-2 and shares with it the IL-2 β and γ receptor (R) subunits. By promoting the activation and proliferation of NK, NK-T, and CD8+ T cells, IL-15 plays important roles in innate and adaptative immunity. Moreover, the association of high levels of IL-15 expression with inflammatory and autoimmune diseases has led to the development of various antagonistic approaches targeting IL-15. This study is an original approach aimed at discovering small-molecule inhibitors impeding IL-15/IL-15R interaction. A pharmacophore and docking-based virtual screening of compound libraries led to the selection of 240 high-scoring compounds, 36 of which were found to bind IL-15, to inhibit the binding of IL-15 to the IL-2Rβ chain or the proliferation of IL-15-dependent cells or both. One of them was selected as a hit and optimized by a structure–activity relationship approach, leading to the first small-molecule IL-15 inhibitor with sub-micromolar activity.

    1 Introduction

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    First identified as a T cell growth factor, interleukin (IL)-15 is a 14–15 kDa cytokine belonging to the IL-2 cytokine family and involved in the differentiation and proliferation of NK and T cells.(1, 2) IL-15 binds a trimeric IL-15 receptor (IL-15R) complex formed by a specific receptor chain (IL-15Rα) combined with the β and γ chains of the IL-2R, acting as signal transducing components. Binding of IL-15 to IL-2Rβ/γ induces Jak1 and Jak3 activation and subsequently phosphorylation of Stat3 and Stat5 (where Jak and Stat stand for Janus kinase and signal transducer and activators of transcription, respectively). High levels of IL-15 expression have been associated with the pathogenesis of autoimmune and inflammatory diseases like Crohn’s disease,(3) psoriasis,(4) leukemias,(5) rheumatoid arthritis (RA),(6) and graft rejection,(7, 8) resulting in the IL-15 system being a target of interest for the treatment of these diseases. Several biological approaches have validated this concept in vivo, including the use of soluble forms of IL-15Rα, antagonist IL-15 mutants, IL-15- or IL-15R-directed monoclonal antibodies and fusion proteins that have proved effective in reducing RA in animal models.(9-11) However, their therapeutic benefit in human diseases has not yet been demonstrated, and further studies and clinical trials are required in human systems to evaluate their safety and efficacy.(12) In this context, the design of small-molecule inhibitors of protein–protein interactions (PPIs) is an emerging and challenging research area,(13, 14) which could be envisaged as an alternative approach to target the IL-15/IL-15R interfaces.
    In fact, potent small-molecule inhibitors of the IL-2/IL-2Rα interface have already been identified using fragment-based approaches(15) or library screening(16) combined with chemistry optimization. Moreover, the targeting of the IL-2Rβ chain by siRNA has been shown to be effective in reducing disease severity in adjuvant-induced arthritis in rats,(17) highlighting the interest of IL-2Rβ as a therapeutic target for the treatment of RA. To date, no small-molecule inhibitor of the IL-15 system has been reported, nor has the targeting of the IL-2Rβ interface been explored by a PPI approach. This work is the first attempt to discover such low MW nonpeptidic inhibitors by targeting the IL-15/IL-2Rβ interface. For this purpose, a pharmacophore model was built (Figure 1) based on IL-15 specific residues(18, 19) involved in this interface and was used to screen virtually commercial and academic chemical databases (ca. 160 000 compounds). The resulting filtered libraries were subsequently docked to the binding site of IL-15 with IL-2Rβ, and the best-docked compounds were retained. On the basis of experimental in vitro properties such as the capacity to bind to IL-15, to reduce the binding of IL-15 to IL-2Rβ, or to inhibit the proliferation of IL-15-dependent cells, one hit was chosen among 240 purchased compounds. After chemical optimization of its structure, this led to the development of a low MW inhibitor of the IL-15/IL-15R system with sub-micromolar efficiency.

    2 Results and Discussion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    2.1 Pharmacophore-Based and Docking-Based Virtual Screening

    In order to disrupt IL-15/IL-15R recognition and impede IL-15 signaling, our approach was to use small MW molecules to target one of the IL-15 domains that are interacting with the signal transducing receptor chains IL-2Rβ or IL-2Rγ. The IL-15/IL-2Rγ interface was set aside to avoid specificity problems due to the sharing of this receptor chain by numerous other cytokine systems (IL-15, IL-2, IL-4, IL-7, IL-9, and IL-21).(20) Thus, we chose to target the IL-15/IL-2Rβ binding domain, a strategy that has not yet been described in the literature.
    Our virtual screening strategy consisted of using pharmacophore searching and docking methods. The 3D structure of IL-15 (PDB code 2Z3Q)(21) was used to generate a pharmacophore model based on three residues, Asp8, Asn65, and Leu69, previously shown by directed-mutagenesis studies(18, 19) or by homology to the IL-2/IL-2Rβ complex(22, 23) to be involved in the binding of IL-15 to IL-2Rβ. Comparison of IL-15 and IL-2 structures after superimposition of IL-15 onto IL-2 in the tetrameric complex structure IL-2–IL-2Rα/β/γ (PDB code 2B5I)(24) (available at the time of this work unlike that of the IL-15 tetrameric complex)(25) led us to consider (a) an additional orientation for the side chain of Asn65, similar to that of its homologous Asn88 in IL-2 (Figure 1A) and (b) the potential participation of two water molecules close to residues Asp8 and Asn65 (Figure 1B). The final model consisted of three main features (one projection of hydrogen bond donor (HBD) and one projection of hydrogen bond donor or acceptor (HBDA) centered on Asp8 and one hydrophobic feature in front of Ile68 and Leu69) completed by ten exclusion spheres centered on the main residues of the defined binding site. In addition, three accessory features (one projection of HBD centered on Asn65, considering two side chain orientations, and finally two projections of HBDA placed on both water molecules) were created (Figure 1C). The pharmacophore model was used to search a database of 160 828 chemical compounds derived from commercial (Chembridge, Maybridge, and Preswick) or academic libraries (National Cancer Institute (NCI) and the French national chemical library Chimiothèque Nationale). To be retained, a hit had to match at least the main features, whereas the presence of the accessory features was not mandatory. A total of 24 115 molecules were returned by the pharmacophore searching and were subjected to docking-based virtual screening using the LigandFit program implemented under Discovery Studio (DS) 3.0 (Accelrys Software Inc., San Diego, CA). More than 30% of the filtered molecules passed the docking stage and were ranked using 5 scoring functions. A consensus strategy was adopted (see Experimental Section), and the 240 top-ranked molecules were purchased (Table S1). They were first tested at a concentration of 100 μM for their ability to bind to IL-15 using the surface plasmon resonance (SPR) technology (Figure 2A) or to inhibit the binding of IL-15 to soluble IL-2Rβ in a homogeneous time resolved fluorescence (HTRF) assay (Figure 2C). Thirty-six compounds were found to bind to IL-15 as well as to our IL-15 superagonist RLI (a fusion protein linking IL-15 to the IL-15Rα sushi domain)(26) (Figure 2A,B), thus discarding any binding of compounds to IL-15 at its interface with IL-15Rα. Some were also shown to reduce the FRET signal induced by the interaction between recombinant IL-2Rβ and IL-15 (Figure 2D). To evaluate their impact on a specific IL-15 functional effect, we further tested these hits for their ability to inhibit IL-15-dependent cell proliferation. Most of them were inhibitors and five of them showed IC50 values around or below 10 μM (Figure S2). One of these, named compound 1 (Figure 3), was selected for chemical optimization. Its binding mode to the IL-15 structure was predicted by a molecular docking technique using LigandFit and C-Docker programs. The interactions(27) involved, as predicted by the last fine docking step, were π–alkyl hydrophobic interactions with residues Ile68 and Leu69, an amide−π stacked interaction with the Ser7 and Asp8 peptidic bond, a hydrogen bond (HB) with Asp8, and a carbon HB with Asn65 (Figure 4A).

    2.2 Synthesis

    Among the identified hits, the chemical structure of compound 1 emerged as a good platform to initiate synthetic modifications to improve its biological activity. The chemical structure of this compound can be regarded as the association of three fragments: a dihydro-phthalazinone tethered to a triazole moiety, which can be considered the main core of the molecule, a terminal aromatic group, and an alkyl linker chain to join both parts of the molecule through S–C bond and amide bond formation (Figure 3). To the best of our knowledge, the synthesis of compound 1 has never been described in the literature, and we began our synthetic work to define the best strategy to access this hit. A good and combinatorial approach consisted of condensing anilines with a carboxylic acid derived from the S-alkylation of the main dihydro-phthalazinone core with bromoacetic acid (Figure 3, route 1). Although this carboxylic acid derivative could easily be prepared with a 60% yield in Williamson-type alkylation conditions,(28) all attempts to access to the amide 1 in common coupling conditions with carbodiimide reagents(29, 30) and 2,4-dichloroaniline failed. We thus turned to a second strategy, exploring the S-alkylation of the dihydro-phthalazinone main core with a variety of halogenoalkylamides previously prepared (Figure 3, route 2).
    The key dihydro-phthalazinone intermediates 4 and 5 were prepared from conjugated ester 2-E obtained by Wittig olefination of phthalic anhydride with the (carbethoxymethylidene)triphenylphosphorane.(31) Successive additions of methylhydrazine and hydrazine(32) in a one-pot, two-step manner afforded the phthalazinone hydrazide 3 in good yield. This latter intermediate was condensed with methyl isothiocyanate(33) to give the mercapto-triazole main intermediate 4 or with CS2 in the presence of KOH to afford oxadiazole 5(34) (Scheme 1).
    Next, halogenoalkylamide linkers 6–24 and 27–38 were obtained from commercially available bromoalkyl carboxylic acids activated as their acid chloride and coupled to the selected amines, mainly substituted anilines but also hydrophobic amines such as 1-adamantylamine or 1-naphthylamine (Scheme 2). To substitute hydrophobic halogenes at the terminal aromatic part of the molecule with hydrophilic substituents, dimethoxy compound 24 was subjected to a demethylation step in the presence of BBr3 to give the phenolic derivatives 25 and 26 (Scheme 3). Because all attempts to prepare the N-methylamide analog 47 later from the hit 1 failed, we had to introduce the N-methyl group earlier by methylation of the alkylating agent 6 with an excess of methyl iodide. This alkylation step was also reluctant and iodo amide 39 was isolated in poor yield (Scheme 2). However, it should be noted that the amide compound 43 bearing a longer alkyl linker chain than 1, could easily be N-methylated in mild conditions (NaH, MeI, DMF, 0 °C) to give derivative 63 in 74% yield.
    In a final step, hit 1 and its analogs 41–54, 56, 64, 66–78, and 86–90 were obtained by S-alkylation of the phathalazinone intermediate 4 with the appropriate bromoalkylamide reagents (Schemes 4 and 5a,b). In some cases, a small amount of N-alkylation of the phthalazinone intermediate 4 could be observed due to the tautomeric properties of the 3-mercapto-1,2,4-triazole ring.(35) In similar conditions, oxadiazole analogs 85 and 91 (Scheme 5c) were prepared starting from the thiol 5.
    Variations at the terminal arylamide part of some compounds were achieved starting from aryl ether or benzoate compounds. Following demethylation of compounds 51 and 74 in the presence of BBr3, phenol intermediates 57 and 79 could react with suitable alkylating agents to give the corresponding phenolic ether analogs 58–60 and 80–82 in good yields (Scheme 6). On the other hand, benzoic acid derivatives 61, 62, and 83 were obtained from hydrolysis of the corresponding esters 52, 53, and 75 with LiOH (Scheme 7).
    Because the 1,2,3-triazole ring system has received considerable interest in medicinal chemistry due to its versatile potential to interact with biological systems through dipolar interactions and hydrogen bonding,(36) we opted for the formation of this heterocycle to replace the terminal amide bond function or to be introduced into a long alkyl chain linker. For this purpose, we prepared the azide 40 (Scheme 2) and the alkyne 55, which were involved in a Huisgen 1,3-dipolar cycloaddition under copper catalysis(37) and gave access to compound 84 with 60% yield. Starting from commercially available 1-azidoadamantane, the intermediate 55 reacted in the same conditions to form compound 56, the 1,2,3-triazole analog of N-1-adamantyl amide 54 (Scheme 8).

    2.3 Structure–Activity Analyses

    To date, to the best of our knowledge, no IL-15 selective inhibitor with low MW has been described in the literature. This work therefore opens the way to the first in vitro SAR study to provide crucial information on the key binding interactions of IL-15 with its IL-2Rβ binding site. Each fragment of compound 1 could be subjected to pertinent modifications, but among the available options, we focused this preliminary work on the study of the functional impact of the length of the alkyl linker and of the structure of the terminal amidic group. The effectiveness of the novel compounds was evaluated using the IL-15-dependent cell proliferation assay and, for the best of them, by SPR technology and the Stat5 phosphorylation assay.
    The binding properties of the compounds were first monitored by SPR. Given that the binding domains of IL-15 and IL-2 with IL-2Rβ show high similarity, we chose to study compound activity systematically on both cytokine systems to provide the opportunity to detect potential selective compounds rapidly. In the SPR assay, the compounds were flowed over a chip’s surface, on which RLI or IL-2 had previously been coupled. Binding of compounds induced a change in mass on the chip, which was detected by the change in reflectance units (RU). Direct interaction of compound 1 with immobilized RLI or IL-2 gave similar Kd values in the 10 μM range (48 and 10 μM, respectively). In the case of RLI, consisting of IL-15 bound to IL-15Rα, the binding of compound 1 could not have occurred on the IL-15 binding domain to IL-15Rα because the latter was no longer accessible.
    For further classic SAR exploration, we preferred to perform a functional and cellular assay with the aim of identifying a potential drug candidate. For this purpose, we first tested our hit 1 analogs, compounds 41–54 and 56–91, for their potency in inhibiting cell proliferation induced by a fixed concentration (close to their respective EC50 values) of RLI (100 pM) or IL-2 (1.5 nM) on 32Dβ cells that express IL-2Rβ/γ. RLI was preferred to IL-15 because of its better efficacy (Figure S3).(38, 39) In addition, the main compounds were checked for their lack of cytotoxicity (Figure S4).

    2.3.1 Probing the Linker to Interact with a Lipophilic Pocket

    Based on our binding model (Figure 4), it appeared that the main phthalazinone-triazole core of compound 1 was involved in major interactions with specific residues of the binding site, such as π–alkyl hydrophobic interactions with Ile68 and Leu69 and carbon HB with Asn65. Furthermore, the model revealed a large hydrophobic patch formed by residues Val3, Ile6, Val104, and Ile111 close to the predicted location of the terminal aromatic group of compound 1, which could be targeted for compound optimization. We reasoned that considering the bis-cyclic aromatic part to be the main anchorage of the hit 1, lengthening the alkyl chain could increase the affinity and activity through additive interactions with the lipophilic channel. We therefore prepared compounds 41–46, which are analogs of compound 1 bearing an alkyl chain with 3–15 methylene groups.
    In the proliferation cellular assay, the IC50 value followed an inverted bell-shaped curve when the length of the alkyl chain was increased (Figure S5), decreasing from 17.3 μM (n = 1) to 1.9 μM (n = 7) and increasing back to 7.3 μM (n = 15) (Table 1). Increasing the alkyl chain therefore led to a gain of 1 order of magnitude in activity potency and identified compounds 43–45 as potential leads (Table 1). We prepared compound 46 with a long chain (n = 15) to test the limit of such chemical modification. Surprisingly, compound 46 kept the activity, suggesting a good adaptation of this long flexible lipophilic chain probably in a folded form. However, no IL-15 versus IL-2 selectivity could be observed at this stage, and the best IC50 values of 1.9 and 1.1 μM for the inhibition of IL-15- and IL-2-induced cell proliferation, respectively, were obtained with compound 43.
    Table 1. In Vitro Inhibition of 32Dβ Cell Proliferation with Compound 1 and Its Homologs 41–46

    2.3.2 SAR Study around the Terminal Amidic Part of the Molecules

    The terminal amidic part of the molecules was predicted to form a stacking interaction between its substituted terminal phenyl ring and the Ser7 and Asp8 peptidic bond and an H bond between its amide function and the carboxyl function of Asp8 of IL-15. Accordingly, the N-methylation of the amide function (Table 2, compound 47) decreased the inhibitory effect. Unexpectedly, the substitution of both 2,4-chloro substituents with various halogens such as bromide or fluoride (Table 2, compounds 48 and 49) gave the same results. We next focused on the para position of the terminal phenyl group, which appeared not far from lipophilic residues such as Ile6 and Val104 in the structure of IL-15 (Figure 4B). We therefore introduced hydrophobic substituents such as iodide (Table 2 and Figure 5A, compound 50) or alkoxy substituents with variable alkyl chains (Table 2, compounds 51, 58–60). To complete the series, we also selected the adamantane moiety as an interesting lipophilic and steric system to substitute the terminal phenyl group (Table 2, compound 54). Uniformly, no improvement in the activity was observed compared to compound 1, and neither with compound 56 (Figure 5A), with a triazole ring in place of the amide function in 54. Finally, to try to favor HB interaction with Lys11, carboxylic and ester functions were introduced into the ortho (compounds 52 and 61) or meta (compounds 53 and 62) positions of the aromatic phenyl cycle but such modifications were detrimental for the activity because no more effect was observed (Figure 5A). In this series, all attempts to improve the biological results failed. Because the IC50 value of the hit compound 1 was close to the limit of sensitivity of the cell proliferation assay, the SAR study of that series of compounds (n = 1) could not be refined up to 30 μM (Figure S6).
    Table 2. SAR Study with Analogs of the Selected Compounds 1, 43, and 45
    Table a

    All values are the mean of at least two independent assays.

    Table b

    Inhibition of 32Dβ cell proliferation induced by RLI (100 pM).

    Table c

    Inhibition of 32Dβ cell proliferation induced by IL-2 (1.5 nM).

    Table d

    Compound 63 was prepared by N-methylation of amide 43 in DMF, in the presence of NaH (1 equiv) and MeI (2 equiv).

    Table e

    Compound 65 was prepared by S-oxidation of thioether 64 in dichloromethane in the presence of 5 equiv of mCPBA.

    Table f

    Compounds were tested in only one experiment in triplicate.

    Table g

    ND, not determined; NE, no effect.

    We then performed modulations on compounds 43 (n = 7) and 45 (n = 10) by varying the terminal amidic part of the molecules. As both compounds had a longer aliphatic linker, it could be expected that this lipophilic part of the molecule would interact more efficiently with the hydrophobic channel formed by residues Val3, Ile111, Ile6, and Val104 of IL-15. The investigation first focused on the 2- and 4-chloro substituents of the phenyl group as explored with hit 1. From the 2,4-dichlorophenyl analog 45 (n = 10), deletion of one chloro substituent in the para (compound 86) or ortho (compound 87) positions of the phenyl group afforded compounds that were three times less potent, which was not observed with the monochloro analog 71 of compound 43 (n = 7), which retained a good antiproliferative activity (IC50 = 0.8 μM, Figure 5B). Substitution of the phenyl ring of 45 with hydrophobic and bulky groups, such as the naphthyl and adamantyl rings retained the activity (Table 2, compounds 88 and 89, Figure 5C), but surprisingly, deletion of this hydrophobic amidic part of the molecule in compound 45 to give the S-decyl analog 90 did not dramatically affect the results (IC50 around 10 μM, Figure 5C).
    We then turned our attention to the SAR on the terminal phenyl group of our best lead compound 43 (n = 7). Replacement of one or both chloro substituents with bromide or fluoride (compounds 64 and 66), an apolar group such as methyl (compounds 67), or a substituent such as methoxy (compounds 68 and 69) was uniformly tolerated by both IL-15 and IL-2 systems, with IC50 values in the micromolar range (Table 2). Deletion of the chloro substituent at the 2-position of the phenyl ring to give the 4-chloro phenyl compound 71 or analogs bearing apolar or polar systems (compounds 72–74) also showed similar activity (Table 2, Figure 5D). The azide analog 73 has been developed as a potential partner for a cross-linking study with the IL-15 protein (data not shown). At this stage, we could conclude that the terminal amidic part of the leads 43 and 45 bearing a long alkyl chain did not appear to be crucial for anchorage on the binding site. Nevertheless, simple N-methylation of the amide function of our lead compound 43 (compound 63) as well as 2- and 4- mono- or disubstitution with a hydroxyl function (compounds 70, 75, and 79), caused an approximately 10-fold decrease in the biological activity (Table 2). These results observed with phenolic compounds suggested that binding to the protein was highly sensitive to acidic functions. This was confirmed with the ester analog 76, whose acidic form 83 showed no effect. Moreover, the ethyl ester 76 was identified as one of the best products tested with an IC50 value of 0.8 μM (Table 2).
    Starting with the inactive phenol analog 79, alkylation with short alkyl chains such as methyl, propyl, isopropyl, or isobutyl restored the activity (compounds 74, 80–82, Table 2 and Figure 5E). Furthermore, as observed with compound 45, substitution of the phenyl ring with a bulky lipophilic substituent such as naphthyl (compounds 77 and 88) or adamantyl (compounds 78 and 89) preserved the activity, and uniformly higher efficiency was observed with analogs with the seven-membered chain (Table 2, Figure 5C).

    2.3.3 Modification of the Mercapto Triazole Part of Compounds 43 and 45

    Suspecting potential biological oxidative events in cellular assays, we reacted the dibromo compound 64 in a chemical oxidation step with meta-chloroperbenzoic acid to afford the sulfone derivative 65. Interestingly, the same results were obtained with the alkylsulfide parent 64 and its sulfone 65, on IL-15 as well as on IL-2. As observed in our binding model (Figure 4), the sulfur atom is surrounded by two asparagine residues, Asn65 and Asn4, which constitute a favorable environment for the oxygen of 65 to make a HB.
    However, in relation to the model, a carbon HB with Asn65 and the methyl group of the N-methyl triazole moiety was predicted. Consequently, we wanted to study the substitution of this N-methyl system with an oxygen atom and so prepared the oxadiazole analogs 85 and 91 of the corresponding triazole compounds 43 and 45. No drastic decrease of the IC50 values was observed, and oxadiazole 85 appeared as one of the best compounds tested, with an IC50 of 0.8 μM on both interleukins (Table 2, Figure 5F). The formation of a HB bond between the amine function of Asn65 residue and the oxygen of the oxadiazole ring could explain this result. Because no IL-15 versus IL-2 selectivity could be observed, we did not go further with the oxadiazole series.

    2.3.4 Measurement of Direct Interaction and Pharmacological Effect of Selected Compounds on IL-15 and IL-2 Systems

    We further analyzed our best compounds using two supplementary cytokine specific assays. We first compared them based on their direct binding to RLI or IL-2 using the SPR technology and then measured their potency in reducing the phosphorylation of Stat5, a signal transducer that is activated when IL-15 or IL-2 binds to its respective receptor expressed at the surface of NK-92 cells (Figure S7).
    The advantage of the p-Stat5 assay over the cell proliferation assay used in the first SAR study is that the readout is obtained over a short time (1 h), close to the initial cytokine receptor binding event, and does not result from the sum of several downstream signaling reactions, occurring in cascade or in parallel. Thus, the p-Stat5 assay represents a specific biological test more adapted to a fine study of SAR in a series of compounds.
    The great majority of the selected compounds displayed Kd values for RLI binding of around 10–20 μM, close to those for IL-2 binding, when measured (Table 3). The compound displaying a somewhat weaker affinity (78 μM) in this series (n = 7) was the adamantyl derivative 78. This was probably due to the bulky nature of the tricyclic moiety, compared to the aromatic phenyl one. Interestingly, lengthening the central alkyl linker to give the adamantyl analog 89 (n = 10) decreased the Kd value to 16 μM, suggesting a better hydrophobic interaction with the binding site on IL-15, possibly near the hydrophobic patch formed with residues Val3, Ile111, Ile6, and Val104.
    Table 3. Pharmacological Data of Selected Potent Compoundsa
    Table a

    All values are the mean of two or more independent assays.

    Table b

    ND = not determined.

    Regarding the in vitro functional p-Stat5 results, the IC50 values measured were mainly around 1 order of magnitude lower than those found in the cell proliferation test (Table 3). This could be explained by the shorter incubation time (1 h versus 2.5 days for p-Stat5 and cell proliferation, respectively), which could have a different impact on compound integrity and efficiency. Overall, these biological results in accordance with those previously obtained in the cell proliferation assay, confirmed the gain in efficacy by lengthening the central alkyl chain of compound 1 or 54 to afford the dichlorophenyl analogs 43 and 45 or the adamantyl analogs 78 and 89, respectively (Figure 5G). Here too, SAR studies with both series (n = 7 and n = 10), did not reveal a unique lead compound (Table 3). However, the best results were obtained with the phenoxy derivatives 80 and 82 and the ethyl ester analog 76 with p-Stat5 inhibitory potencies of around 50 nM (Table 3). In the ether series, compounds with alkyl chains having fewer than three extended carbons with or without ramification, such as methoxy 74 and isopropoxy 81, were the least efficient (Figure 5H). The ester 76 could be classified as the best compound in this study with p-Stat5 inhibitory efficiencies of 57 and 43 nM with IL-15 and IL-2 respectively, and a cell proliferation IC50 of 0.8 μM with both cytokines.
    Taken together, these results led to the discovery of the first small-molecule inhibitors of the IL-15 system showing efficacies in the 50 nM range in a p-Stat5 assay and in the micromolar range in a cell proliferation assay as observed with the ester 76 and the alkoxy derivatives 80 and 82. These compounds also proved to be effective on the IL-2 system. It should be noted that similar affinity or activity values were described in earlier studies for nonpeptidic small-molecule inhibitors of IL-2, in that case targeting specifically the IL-2/IL-2Rα interface.(15, 16, 40, 41)
    Ongoing clinical trials are being conducted with humanized Mik-Beta-1 antibody (Hu-Mik-Beta1) directed against IL-2Rβ, which targets both IL-15 and IL-2 systems. This antibody is currently being tested in patients with arthritis, celiac disease, multiple sclerosis, and HTLV-1-associated myelopathy/tropical spastic paraparesis (HAM/TSP)(42, 43) (clinicaltrials.gov) and has also been previously shown to prolong primate cardiac allograft survival.(44) Taking advantage of their combined profile, the kind of inhibitors described in this study may be of therapeutic value in pathologies where both cytokines have been shown to be involved such as RA,(17) HAM/TSP, inflammatory bowel disease, or multiple sclerosis.(45)

    3 Conclusion

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To
    In the present study, we report the development process of hit optimization from structure-based drug virtual screening to small-molecule compounds blocking the interaction of IL-15 to its receptor with sub-micromolar efficiency. This is the first and successful attempt to target the IL-15 system by a PPI inhibitor approach, which opens the way for the identification of new classes of therapeutic drugs for IL-15 related pathologies such as RA or graft rejection. Binding and biological cell proliferation assays led to the selection of one hit among a number of compounds initially selected by virtual screening. This hit has an inhibitory activity in the 10 μM range and was used as a starting template for lead discovery. Pharmacomodulation studies around the linker chain and the terminal amidic part of the hit led to inhibitors with IC50 values close to 50 nM in cell-based experiments, nevertheless showing the same activity on the IL-2 system. Having now identified the first class of small-molecule inhibitors of the IL-15/IL15R system, we are currently investigating the development of analogs with higher IL-15 selectivity, to rule out possible side effects to specific IL-2 blockade, such as the decrease of regulatory T cells.

    4 Experimental Section

    ARTICLE SECTIONS
    Jump To

    4.1 Computational Study

    4.1.1 Pharmacophore Modeling

    At the time of this work, the structure of IL-15 in complex with IL-2Rβ was not yet available. The X-ray crystal structure of IL-15 extracted from the structure of the IL-15/IL-15Rα complex (PDB code 2Z3Q)(21) was thus used. The protein structure was first prepared by adding the hydrogen atoms, removing the water molecules, and inserting the missing loop regions using the Prepare Protein tool within DS3.0. After a series of energy minimizations carried out with CHARMM force field and Steepest Descent algorithm implemented into DS3.0, the protein was used for generating a pharmacophore model using the Structure Based Pharmacophore tool within DS3.0. Pharmacophore models were created from the binding site of IL-15 with IL-15Rβ based on “hot spot” residues (Asp8, Asn65, and Leu69). The superimposition of IL-15 onto IL-2 in the tetrameric complex structure IL-2–IL-2Rα/β/γ (PDB code 2B5I)(24) had led us to consider an additional orientation for the side chain of residue Asn65 of IL-15, similar to that of its homologous IL-2 residue Asn88. Moreover, the X-ray crystal structure of IL-2–IL-2R complex shows two water molecules involved in the hydrogen bonding networks between Asp20 and Asn88 (IL-2 counterpart of Asp8 and Asn65 of IL-15) and the side chains of IL-2Rβ. Thus, two water molecules close to residues Asp8 and Asn65 of IL-15 were also taken into consideration. The pharmacophore model was built using hydrogen bond donor (HBD), hydrogen bond donor and acceptor (HBDA), and HY (hydrophobic) features on the basis of the four IL-15 residues, Asp8, Asn65, Ile68 and Leu69. Two HBD projections were centered on OD1 atoms within residues Asp8 and Asn65 (two orientations of its side chain considered) and a HBDA projection on OD2 atom within residue Asp8, and all were located within a sphere of 2.2 Å radius. The HY feature was manually created at distances of 5.062, 4.554, and 6.346 Å from carbon α (CA) atoms of residues Ile68, Leu69, and Asn65, respectively. Exclusion spheres of 1.6 Å radius were generated, centered on carbon β (CB) atoms of residues Asn1, Asn4, Ser7, Asp8, Thr62, Asn65, Ile68, Leu69, and Asn72 and on carbon γ (CG) atom of Lys11. Two HBDA projections were placed on water molecules 147 and 148. Finally, virtual screening was done using as main features HBD and HBDA projections on Asp8, the HY feature, and the exclusion spheres and as accessory features the HBD projection on Asn65 and HBDA projections on water molecules. A hit had to match at least the main features while the presence of the accessory features was not mandatory.

    4.1.2 Virtual Screening

    The chemical library for virtual screening was obtained by merging commercial (Chembridge, Maybridge, Preswick) and academic collections (the Diversity set II from the NCI and the French national chemical library Chimiothèque Nationale). Duplicate structures were removed and 3D coordinates (using Catalyst) were generated using the Prepare ligand tool within DS3.0. A multiconformation ligand database was then created using Catalyst within the Build 3D Database tool under DS3.0. The query was performed using the Search 3D Database tool with the FAST search method under DS3.0. Hits were screened for Fit values, and those with a Fit value more than 1 were then taken for molecular docking analysis.

    4.1.3 Molecular Docking

    Molecular docking studies were performed using LigandFit option of receptor–ligand interactions protocol section available in DS3.0. Initially the protein was prepared by adding the hydrogen atoms, removing the water molecules, and inserting the missing loop regions and then was minimized using CHARMM as described above. The protein molecule thus prepared and minimized was defined as the total receptor. Four docking conditions (4 targets: IL-15 protein with residue Asn65 side chain in its initial orientation (as referred to structure 2Z3Q) or in an orientation close to that of its IL-2 homologue Asn88 (from structure 2B5I), as well as each of these targets with 2 water molecules) were conducted in parallel. For each condition, the ligand molecules retained by the pharmacophore model were docked into a binding site facing residues Asn1, Asn4, Ser7, Asp8, Lys11, Thr62, Asn65, Ile68, Leu69, and Asn72 of IL-15 structures. The volumes of the binding site were 1396, 1538, 1481, and 1523 Å3 and contained 11171, 12304, 11851, and 12188 points, respectively, to the 4 targets described above. Docking was performed using CFF as the energy grid. A penalty of 200 kcal/(mol·atom) was set up to reduce the dock score of poses that occurred outside of the binding site. The conformational search of the ligand poses was performed by the Monte Carlo trial method. Maximum internal energy was set at 10000 kcal/mol. A short rigid body minimization was then performed (steepest descent and Broyden–Fletcher–Goldfarb–Shanno (BFGS) minimizations). Twenty poses were saved for each ligand after docking, and 100 steps of BFGS rigid body minimization were then carried out. Scoring was performed with five scoring functions: LigScore1, Ligscore2,(46) PLP1, PLP2,(47) and PMF.(48, 49) CFF force field was used for LigScore calculations. Among the 24115 compounds retained after the pharmacophore-based search, 7991 were actually docked to the targets. To improve the screening accuracy, a consensus strategy was adopted after dividing compounds in subsets based on MW (MW ≤600 and >600 Da) and number of rotatable bonds (rotatable bonds ≤5 and >5). Compounds with a consensus score equal to 5 (i.e., in the TOP 40% for each scoring function) were retained within each subset for each docking condition. All together, 1588 compounds were thereby retained. They were ranked by rescoring them against each of the 4 targets and summing the four consensus scores obtained (maximal score = 20). The top-ranked 20% were selected and represented 315 compounds, 240 of which were actually available and purchased for biological testing.

    4.1.4 Binding Mode and Molecular Interactions Analysis of Compound 1

    In order to determine the potential binding mode of compound 1, we performed a refinement step on the top 50 poses obtained from two more docking runs, one using LigandFit and the other using C-Docker, another docking program implemented under DS3.0. This refinement procedure consisted in a simulated annealing refinement (2000 steps at 700 K, 5000 steps at 300 K) followed by a final full force field minimization using CHARMM (100 steps of steepest descent followed by conjugate gradient with gradient tolerance of 0.01). The 100 poses retained with the two docking runs were compared in terms of interaction energy, and those with the most favorable binding were further visually validated for their proper docking. The top 10 poses belonged to the same cluster, and one of them has been selected.
    Images for the Figures 1 and 4 were captured from DS3.0 visualizer and treated with Adobe Photoshop CS4 software.

    4.2 Chemistry

    4.2.1 General

    Melting points were determined on a Stuart melting point SMP3 apparatus. All solvents used were reagent grade, and TLC was performed on silica-covered aluminum sheets (Kieselgel 60F254, MERCK). Eluted TLC was revealed using UV radiation (λ = 254 nm) or molybdate solution. Flash column chromatography was performed on silica gel 60 ACC 40–63 μm (SDS-CarloErba). NMR spectra were recorded on a BRUKER AC300 (300 MHz for 1H and 75 MHz for 13C) or on a BRUKER 400 (400 MHz for 1H and 100 MHz for 13C) at room temperature on samples dissolved in an appropriate deuterated solvent. References of tetramethylsilane (TMS) for 1H and deuterated solvent signal for 13C were used. Chemical displacement values (δ) are expressed in parts per million (ppm) and coupling constants (J) in Hertz (Hz). Clearly identified proton and carbon were specified (for dihydrophthalazinone moiety as phthal and for triazole moiety as triaz). Low-resolution mass spectra (MS in Da unit) were recorded in the CEISAM laboratory on a Thermo-Finnigan DSQII quadripolar at 70 eV (CI with NH3 gas) or on a Waters Xevo G2-XS QTOF. High-resolution mass spectrometry (HRMS in Da unit) analyses were recorded on an LC-Q-TOF (Synapt-G2 HDMS, Waters) in the IRS-UN center (Mass Spectrometry platform, Nantes) or on a MALDI -TOF-TOF apparatus (Autoflex III from Bruker) in the INRA center (BIBS platform, Nantes). The purity of the tested compounds was determined by analytical UPLC-MS (Acquity H-Class- Xevo G2-XS Qtof) using C18 reverse-phase column [BEH (1.7 μm, 2.1 mm × 50 mm)] and was over 95%. The compounds described in Tables 1–3 were checked for the presence of PAINS substructures as described by Baell and Holloway,(50) using FAF-Drugs3(51) server, and no PAINS substructures were identified except in 73, which was only developed for the purpose of carrying out cross-linking studies with IL-15 protein.
    Synthesis and chemical characteristics of the halogenoalkylamides 6–39 and the azidoalkylamide 40 are detailed in the Supporting Information.

    4.2.2 General Synthetic Procedure 1 for the Synthesis of Bromoalkylamides

    The commercial bromoacid reagent (1 equiv) was dissolved in anhydrous DCM, and oxalyl chloride (1.2 equiv) was added at room temperature. A catalytic amount of DMF was then added. The mixture was stirred under argon atmosphere during 3 h. Carboxylic acid complete conversion to acyl chloride was determined by 1H NMR in deuterated benzene after concentration in vacuum. The acyl chloride was taken up in anhydrous THF, and the amine (0.85 equiv) was then added at 0 °C, followed by triethylamine (2.5 equiv). After completion of the reaction, water was added, and the aqueous phase was extracted with Et2O (the aqueous layer was acidified to pH = 2 for compounds with phenol function). The organic phase was washed with brine, dried over MgSO4, and concentrated under reduced pressure. The expected bromoalkylamide product was then purified by flash column chromatography on silica gel.

    4.2.3 General Synthetic Procedure 2 for the S-Alkylation of 4-((5-Mercapto-4-methyl-4H-1,2,4-triazol-3-yl)methyl)-2-methylphthalazin-1(2H)-one (4) and 4-((5-Mercapto-1,3,4-oxadiazol-2-yl)methyl)-2-methylphthalazin-1(2H)-one (5) with Halogenoalkyles or Halogenoalkylamides To Give Compounds 1, 41–54, 64, 66–78, and 86–91

    To a suspension of dry K2CO3 (3 equiv, 3 mmol) in DMF (8 mL) was added thiol 4 (1 mmol). The mixture was stirred for 15 min, and a solution of bromoalkylamide (1.5 equiv) in CH2Cl2 (1 mmol·mL–1) was added. The mixture was then stirred at room temperature until starting material disappeared (2–18 h). After completion of the reaction, a saturated solution of ammonium chloride was added. The mixture was extracted twice with ethyl acetate. The organic layer was washed several times with brine, dried over MgSO4, and concentrated under reduced pressure. The crude was purified by flash chromatography on silica gel.

    4.2.4 N-(2,4-Dichlorophenyl)-2-((4-methyl-5-((3-methyl-4-oxo-3,4-dihydrophthalazin-1-yl)methyl)-4H-1,2,4-triazol-3-yl)thio)acetamide (1)

    4.2.23 4-((5-(((1-((1S,3S)-Adamantan-1-yl)-1H-1,2,3-triazol-4-yl)methyl)thio)-4-methyl-4H-1,2,4-triazol-3-yl)methyl)-2-methylphthalazin-1(2H)-one (56)

    4.3 Biological Assays

    4.3.1 Cell Lines, Recombinant Proteins, and Antibodies

    The 32Dβ and NK-92 cell lines were used in that study and cultured as described previously.(38, 52) Recombinant human IL-15 and recombinant murine IL-3 were obtained from Peprotech, Inc., and recombinant human IL-2 was from Chiron. RLI was produced as described previously.(26) Recombinant IL-2Rβ (224-2B-025/CF) was purchased from R&D Systems. CF1 antibody was from Beckman Coulter.

    4.3.2 SPR Experiment

    The SPR experiments were performed at 25 °C with a BIAcore 3000 biosensor (GE Healthcare, Chalfont St Giles, UK). Recombinant IL-15, RLI, or IL-2 was covalently immobilized to CM5 sensor chips using the amine coupling method in accordance with the manufacturer’s instructions, and the binding of compounds at 100 μM or at increasing concentrations prepared in PBS or 1% or 5% DMSO was monitored. Analysis of sensorgrams was performed using BIAeval 4.1 software.

    4.3.3 HTRF Assays

    HTRF assays were performed using as donor molecule Lumi4-Tb-NHS cryptate (Tb) conjugated to CF1 mouse anti-human IL-2Rβ mAb and as acceptor molecule D2 conjugated to streptavidin (streptavidin–D2) (HTRF Dye labeling kits, Cisbio). The donor excitation at 320 nm leads to energy emission at 620 nm that induces in close proximity (d < 10 nm) excitation of the acceptor and energy emission at 665 nm. The assays were carried out in a white 384-well Small Volume HiBase polystyrene microplate (Greiner) at room temperature. After a 30 min preincubation period of biotinylated human IL-15 (Fluorokine, R&D Systems) with compounds, recombinant human IL-2Rβ was added for an additional 30 min incubation time. Finally, the donor CF1–Tb and the acceptor streptavidin–D2 were added to the mixture and incubated for 30 min. Final concentrations were 100 μM for compounds and 20 nM for biotinylated IL-15, IL-2Rβ, CF1–Tb, and streptavidin–D2 in PBS, 0.1% BSA, 1% DMSO. Fluorescence signals were read with a Mithras reader (Berthold) at excitation 320 nm and emission 620 and 665 nm, and the HTRF was calculated as normalized fluorescence transfer value (ΔF):

    4.3.4 Proliferation Assays

    The proliferation response of 32Dβ cells to RLI was assessed by Alamar blue reduction assay (AbDSerotec). Cells were starved in the culture medium without cytokine for 4 h. Cells (1 × 104) were cultured for 2.5 days in the medium supplemented with 100 pM RLI or 1.5 nM IL-2, previously preincubated for 30 min with fixed or increasing concentrations of compounds or the vehicle (culture medium, DMSO 0.1%, final concentration). Alamar blue (10 μL) was added to each well, and after a 6 h-incubation period at 37 °C, the emitted fluorescence at 590 nm under excitation at 560 nm was measured using Fluoroskan Ascent FL reader (Thermo Electro Corporation). Compounds were tested in triplicate in at least three independent experiments.

    4.3.5 P-STAT5 Assays

    Exponentially growing NK-92 cells were washed and serum-starved overnight to reduce basal phosphorylation. Cells (2 × 105) were then stimulated at 37 °C for 1 h with fixed or increasing concentrations of IL-15 or IL-2 that have or have not been preincubated for 30 min with increasing concentrations of chemical compounds or the vehicle (PBS, BSA 0.1% DMSO 0.6%, final concentration). At the end of the stimulation, cells were lysed, and Stat5 phosphorylation was measured according to the manufacturer’s instructions using p-Stat5 AlphaScreen Surefire kit (PerkinElmer Life Sciences). Compounds were tested in duplicate in at least three independent experiments.

    4.3.6 Cytotoxicity Assay

    The cytotoxicity of the main compounds was evaluated on 32Dβ cells using the CytoTox-FluorTM Cytoxicity Assay kit from Promega. Briefly, 1 × 104 cells were plated in 100 μL and cultured in the medium supplemented with vehicle (culture medium, DMSO 0.1%, final concentration) or compounds at a concentration of 11 μM for 3 h. The bis-AAF-R110 substrate was then added in each well, and the dead-cell protease activity was detected 30 min later by measuring the fluorescence signals with a Mithras reader (Berthold) at excitation 485 nm and emission 520 nm. Conditions with medium and with medium supplemented with digitonin were used for negative and positive controls, respectively. Compounds were tested in triplicate in two independent experiments.

      3
      Increased numbers of interleukin-15-expressing cells in active ulcerative colitis
      Kirman I; Nielsen O H
      The American journal of gastroenterology (1996), 91 (9), 1789-94 ISSN:0002-9270.
      OBJECTIVES: Interleukin-15 (IL-15) is a novel cytokine sharing many of the activities of IL-2. The goal of this study was to evaluate intracellular and serum IL-15 in ulcerative colitis (UC) and Crohn's disease (CD). METHODS: Intracellular expression of IL-15 in peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMC) from UC patients, CD patients, and controls was studied using cell permeabilization and staining with monoclonal antibodies. Serum levels of IL-15 were detected using ELISA. RESULTS: Percentage of IL-15 expressing PBMC was increased in UC patients and in five of six of CD patients with moderate and severe disease activity compared with controls. The number of IL-15 expressing cells in patients with active inflammatory bowel disease (IBD) declined within 2 wk of treatment. Serum IL-15 reached detectable levels in 62.5% of UC patients with moderate and severe disease activity but not in UC patients with slight disease activity or in remission, neither in CD patients nor in controls. In vitro lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-induced activation of PBMC from controls was associated with up-regulation of intracellular IL-15 expression (p < 0.01) and release of IL-15. CONCLUSIONS: UC patients with moderate and severe disease activity have increased percentage of IL-15 expressing PBMC, which might be induced by in vivo cell activation and can lead to elevation of released IL-15 in serum. Increased IL-15 expression after in vitro LPS stimulation of control PBMC suggests a nonspecific production of this cytokine during the immunoinflammatory response.

      5
      Pathological roles of interleukin-15 in adult T-cell leukemia
      Yamada, Yasuaki; Kamihira, Shimeru
      Leukemia & Lymphoma (1999), 35 (1/2), 37-45CODEN: LELYEA; ISSN:1042-8194. (Harwood Academic Publishers)
      A review with 63 refs. Interleukin-15 (IL-15) is a 14-kDa glycoprotein belonging to the same 4 helix bundle-cytokine family as IL-2. Although the biol. activity of IL-15 in vitro has been studied extensively, its physiol. role is still obscure. The receptors for IL-15 and IL-2 consist of 3 subunits, an unique α chain and shared β and γ chains. Since β and γ chains transduce the signals from the receptors, both cytokines share similar biol. functions, although IL-15 and IL-2 do not have sequence homol. The α chains function to increase binding affinity for each cytokines. Adult T-cell leukemia (ATL) cells constitutively express the IL-2 receptor (IL-2R) α and expression is believed to be essential in the early stages of disease development. We have recently shown that ATL cells also express the complete form of IL-15R including the α chain, and that ATL cells proliferate in response to exogenous IL-15. Since the mRNA of IL-15 is ubiquitous and is detected in many tissues and cells, it is possible that IL-15R stimulation is involved in the development and progression of ATL. Here, we review recent studies on IL-15 and IL-15R and their assocn. with ATL and other lymphoproliferative disorders.

      Interleukin 15: a proinflammatory role in rheumatoid arthritis synovitis

      A review with 32 refs. In rheumatoid arthritis (RA), locally produced interleukin 15 (IL-15) can recruit and activate synovial T cells, which then amplify and perpetuate inflammation through induction of monocyte-derived tumor necrosis factor α (TNF-α) via a cell-contact-dependent mechanism. Thus, IL-15 could play a pivotal role in disease pathogenesis, acting upstream of TNF-α in orchestrating the induction of a cascade of inflammatory cytokines.

      Interleukin-15 gene transcripts are present in rejecting islet allografts
      Manfro, R. C.; Roy-Chaudhury, P.; Zheng, X. X.; Steiger, J.; Nickerson, P. W.; Li, Y.; Maslinski, W.; Strom, T. B.

      Greater interleukin-15 gene expression was present in murine pancreatic islet allografts than in murine syngeneic islet grafts. Apparently, factors other than interleukin-2 are able to mediate allograft rejection in vivo.

      Wang, D.; Deng, X.; Leng, X.; Mao, X. Interleukin-15 receptor-directed immunotoxins atteunuate disease severity in rat adjuvant arthritis Mol. Immunol. 2010, 47, 1535– 1543 DOI: 10.1016/j.molimm.2010.01.023

      11
      Interleukin-15 receptor-directed immunotoxins attenuate disease severity in rat adjuvant arthritis
      Wang, Deling; Deng, Xianglei; Leng, Xiaoyan; Mao, Xiaohua
      Molecular Immunology (2010), 47 (7-8), 1535-1543CODEN: MOIMD5; ISSN:0161-5890. (Elsevier Ltd.)
      The authors previously constructed two Pseudomonas exotoxin (PE)-based immunotoxins, IL15-PEΔ293 (IT1) and IL15M-PEΔ293 (IT2), for eliminating interleukin-15 receptor (IL-15R)-overexpressing cells. These two immunotoxins were generated by fusing either wild-type human IL-15 or an antagonist mutant IL-15 (IL-15M) to a modified form of PE. In this study the anti-arthritic effect of IT1 and IT2 was investigated using the rat model of adjuvant arthritis (AA). The authors found that both IT1 and IT2 could specifically target IL-15R-pos. cells and induce apoptosis. After AA induction, treatment with either IT1 or IT2 resulted in profound improvement of the disease, with redns. in levels of synovial mononuclear leukocytes and certain inflammatory factors. Clin. and histol. comparisons, together with the analyses of mRNA expression and the signal transducer and activator of transcription-3 (STAT-3) phosphorylation, revealed that the two immunotoxins decreased joint inflammation in AA rats to a similar extent. These data suggest that eliminating IL-15R-bearing cells via immunotoxins may be a promising approach for RA treatment. In addn., wild-type IL-15-based immunotoxins can be as effective as antagonist mutant IL-15-based immunotoxins in preventing joint inflammation assocd. with rheumatoid arthritis.

      Therapeutic potential of IL-15 in rheumatoid arthritis
      Yang, Xiao-Ke; Xu, Wang-Dong; Leng, Rui-Xue; Liang, Yan; Liu, Yan-Yan; Fang, Xin-Yu; Feng, Chen-Chen; Li, Rui; Cen, Han; Pan, Hai-Feng; Ye, Dong-Qing
      Human Immunology (2015), 76 (11), 812-818CODEN: HUIMDQ; ISSN:0198-8859. (Elsevier B.V.)
      Rheumatoid arthritis (RA) is a chronic, destructive inflammatory autoimmune disease. Cytokine-mediated immunity has been found to play an important role in the pathogenesis of autoimmune diseases including RA. Recently, much attention has been paid on the role of IL-15, which is a member of the 4 α-helix bundle cytokine family. IL-15 was detected in serum and synovial fluid from RA patients and arthritis mice models. Moreover, administration of IL-15 leads to the development of severe inflammatory arthritis, suggesting that IL-15 may be therapeutically relevant in RA. Therefore, targeting IL-15 may be significantly important and valuable. In this article, we discuss the biol. features and effects of IL-15 and summarize recent advances on the pathol. roles of IL-15 in RA and treatment for RA.

      Sheng, C.; Dong, G.; Miao, Z.; Zhang, W.; Wang, W. State-of-the-art strategies for targeting protein-protein interactions by small-molecule inhibitors Chem. Soc. Rev. 2015, 44, 8238– 8259 DOI: 10.1039/C5CS00252D
      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar

      State-of-the-art strategies for targeting protein-protein interactions by small-molecule inhibitors
      Sheng, Chunquan; Dong, Guoqiang; Miao, Zhenyuan; Zhang, Wannian; Wang, Wei
      Chemical Society Reviews (2015), 44 (22), 8238-8259CODEN: CSRVBR; ISSN:0306-0012. (Royal Society of Chemistry)
      Targeting protein-protein interactions (PPIs) has emerged as a viable approach in modern drug discovery. However, the identification of small mols. enabling us to effectively interrupt their interactions presents significant challenges. In the recent past, significant advances have been made in the development of new biol. and chem. strategies to facilitate the discovery process of small-mol. PPI inhibitors. This review aims to highlight the state-of-the-art technologies and the achievements made recently in this field. The "hot spots" of PPIs have been proved to be crit. for small mols. to bind. Three strategies including screening, designing, and synthetic approaches have been explored for discovering PPI inhibitors by targeting the "hot spots". Although the classic high throughput screening approach can be used, fragment screening, fragment-based drug design and newly improved virtual screening are demonstrated to be more effective in the discovery of PPI inhibitors. In addn. to screening approaches, design strategies including anchor-based and small mol. mimetics of secondary structures involved in PPIs have become powerful tools as well. Finally, constructing new chem. spaced libraries with high diversity and complexity is becoming an important area of interest for PPI inhibitors. The successful cases from the recent five year studies are used to illustrate how these approaches are implemented to uncover and optimize small mol. PPI inhibitors and notably some of them have become promising therapeutics.

      Drug-Like Protein-Protein Interaction Modulators: Challenges and Opportunities for Drug Discovery and Chemical Biology
      Villoutreix, Bruno O.; Kuenemann, Melaine A.; Poyet, Jean-Luc; Bruzzoni-Giovanelli, Heriberto; Labbe, Celine; Lagorce, David; Sperandio, Olivier; Miteva, Maria A.
      Molecular Informatics (2014), 33 (6-7), 414-437CODEN: MIONBS; ISSN:1868-1743. (Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA)
      A review. Fundamental processes in living cells are largely controlled by macromol. interactions and among them, protein-protein interactions (PPIs) have a crit. role while their dysregulations can contribute to the pathogenesis of numerous diseases. Although PPIs were considered as attractive pharmaceutical targets already some years ago, they have been thus far largely unexploited for therapeutic interventions with low mol. wt. compds. Several limiting factors, from technol. hurdles to conceptual barriers, are known, which, taken together, explain why research in this area has been relatively slow. However, this last decade, the scientific community has challenged the dogma and became more enthusiastic about the modulation of PPIs with small drug-like mols. In fact, several success stories were reported both, at the preclin. and clin. stages. In this review article, written for the 2014 International Summer School in Chemoinformatics (Strasbourg, France), we discuss in silico tools (essentially post 2012) and databases that can assist the design of low mol. wt. PPI modulators (these tools can be found at www.vls3d.com). We first introduce the field of protein-protein interaction research, discuss key challenges and comment recently reported in silico packages, protocols and databases dedicated to PPIs. Then, we illustrate how in silico methods can be used and combined with exptl. work to identify PPI modulators.

      In vitro and in silico exploration of IL-2 inhibition by small drug-like molecules
      Kalsoom, Saima; Rashid, Umer; Shaukat, Awais; Abdalla, Omer Mohamed; Hussain, Khalida; Khan, Waqasuddin; Nazir, Samina; Mesaik, Mohammad Ahmad; Zaheer-ul-Haq; Ansari, Farzana Latif
      Medicinal Chemistry Research (2013), 22 (12), 5739-5751CODEN: MCREEB; ISSN:1054-2523. (Birkhaeuser Boston)
      Interleukin-2 (IL-2) is an immunoregulatory cytokine produced by T lymphocytes in response to antigen. It is a potent growth and differentiation factor for several cell-types and is structurally related to the four-helix bundle family of cytokines. Here, we report IL-2 inhibitory potential and computational studies on different series of chalcones, benzothiazepines, semicarbazones, and dihydropyrimidines. These compds. were synthesized in wet lab and were then tested for their potency as IL-2 inhibitors through in vitro T cell proliferation, IL-2 cytokine prodn. as well as their effect on oxidative burst. Compds. that showed significant suppressive activity were further evaluated for their cytotoxicity on normal two cell lines. Most of the chalcones were found to have a powerful inhibitory effect on T-lymphocytes proliferation and cytokine prodn. Among the aza heterocycles benzothiazepines, benzoxazepines, and benzodiazepinones were found to be the strongest IL-2 inhibitors. Mol. docking and MD simulation studies were carried out to correlate exptl. and theor. results whereby a good correlation was obsd. which indicated that computational studies could provide an alternate tool for the identification and designing of more potent IL-2 inhibitors.

      Crystal structure of the IL-15-IL-15Rα complex, a cytokine-receptor unit presented in trans
      Chirifu, Mami; Hayashi, Chiharu; Nakamura, Teruya; Toma, Sachiko; Shuto, Tsuyoshi; Kai, Hirofumi; Yamagata, Yuriko; Davis, Simon J.; Ikemizu, Shinji
      Nature Immunology (2007), 8 (9), 1001-1007CODEN: NIAMCZ; ISSN:1529-2908. (Nature Publishing Group)
      Interleukin 15 (IL-15) and IL-2, which promote the survival of memory CD8+ T cells and regulatory T cells, resp., bind receptor complexes that share β- and γ-signaling subunits. Receptor specificity is provided by unique, nonsignaling α-subunits. Whereas IL-2 receptor-α (IL-2Rα) is expressed together in cis with the β- and γ-subunits on T cells and B cells, IL-15Rα is expressed in trans on antigen-presenting cells. Here we present a 1.85-Å crystal structure of the human IL-15-IL-15Rα complex. The structure provides insight into the mol. basis of the specificity of cytokine recognition and emphasizes the importance of water in generating this very high-affinity complex. Despite very low IL-15-IL-2 sequence homol. and distinct receptor architecture, the topologies of the IL-15-IL-15Rα and IL-2-IL-2Rα complexes are very similar. Our data raise the possibility that IL-2, like IL-15, might be capable of being presented in trans in the context of its unique receptor α-chain.

      Mechanistic and structural insight into the functional dichotomy between IL-2 and IL-15
      Ring, Aaron M.; Lin, Jian-Xin; Feng, Dan; Mitra, Suman; Rickert, Mathias; Bowman, Gregory R.; Pande, Vijay S.; Li, Peng; Moraga, Ignacio; Spolski, Rosanne; Uezkan, Engin; Leonard, Warren J.; Garcia, K. Christopher
      Nature Immunology (2012), 13 (12), 1187-1195CODEN: NIAMCZ; ISSN:1529-2908. (Nature Publishing Group)
      Interleukin 15 (IL-15) and IL-2 have distinct immunol. functions even though both signal through the receptor subunit IL-2Rβ and the common γ-chain (γc). Here we found that in the structure of the IL-15-IL-15Rα-IL-2Rβ-γc quaternary complex, IL-15 binds to IL-2Rβ and γc in a heterodimer nearly indistinguishable from that of the IL-2-IL-2Rα-IL-2Rβ-γc complex, despite their different receptor-binding chemistries. IL-15Rα substantially increased the affinity of IL-15 for IL-2Rβ, and this allostery was required for IL-15 trans signaling. Consistent with their identical IL-2Rβ-γc dimer geometries, IL-2 and IL-15 showed similar signaling properties in lymphocytes, with any differences resulting from disparate receptor affinities. Thus, IL-15 and IL-2 induced similar signals, and the cytokine specificity of IL-2Rα vs. IL-15Rα detd. cellular responsiveness. Our results provide new insights for the development of specific immunotherapeutics based on IL-15 or IL-2.

      Alkylsulfanyl-1,2,4-triazoles, a New Class of Allosteric Valosine Containing Protein Inhibitors. Synthesis and Structure-Activity Relationships
      Polucci, Paolo; Magnaghi, Paola; Angiolini, Mauro; Asa, Daniela; Avanzi, Nilla; Badari, Alessandra; Bertrand, Jay; Casale, Elena; Cauteruccio, Silvia; Cirla, Alessandra; Cozzi, Liviana; Galvani, Arturo; Jackson, Peter K.; Liu, Yichin; Magnuson, Steven; Malgesini, Beatrice; Nuvoloni, Stefano; Orrenius, Christian; Sirtori, Federico Riccardi; Riceputi, Laura; Rizzi, Simona; Trucchi, Beatrice; O'Brien, Tom; Isacchi, Antonella; Donati, Daniele; D'Alessio, Roberto
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (2013), 56 (2), 437-450CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      Valosine contg. protein (VCP), also known as p97, is a member of AAA ATPase family that is involved in several biol. processes and plays a central role in the ubiquitin-mediated degrdn. of misfolded proteins. VCP is an ubiquitously expressed, highly abundant protein and has been found overexpressed in many tumor types, sometimes assocd. with poor prognosis. In this respect, VCP has recently received a great deal of attention as a potential new target for cancer therapy. In this paper, the discovery and structure-activity relationships of alkylsulfanyl-1,2,4-triazoles, a new class of potent, allosteric VCP inhibitors, are described. Medicinal chem. manipulation of 3-(isopropylthio)-5-(phenoxymethyl)-4-phenyl-1,2,4-triazole, identified via HTS, led to the discovery of potent and selective inhibitors with submicromolar activity in cells and clear mechanism of action at consistent doses. This represents a first step toward a new class of potential anticancer agents.

      29
      The diisopropylcarbodiimide/1-hydroxy-7-azabenzotriazole system: segment coupling and stepwise peptide assembly
      Carpino, Louis A.; El-Faham, Ayman
      Tetrahedron (1999), 55 (22), 6813-6830CODEN: TETRAB; ISSN:0040-4020. (Elsevier Science Ltd.)
      For a group of model peptide segments, coupling reactions carried out using soln.- or solid-phase techniques have demonstrated the advantages of DIC/HOAt system over DIC/HOBt, and also, for systems involving other selected carbodiimides and substituted HOBt derivs. bearing electron-withdrawing substituents. Very little, if any, loss of configuration occurred in DCM (dichloromethane) regardless of the additive used, although the relative order of efficiency was similar in solvents such as DMF in which more extensive epimerization resulted. Using DIC/HOAt system for stepwise peptide assembly by solid-phase techniques, it was found that the hindered pyridine base 2,4,6-collidine enhanced the step involving preactivation of the carboxylic acid residue in contrast to the normal situation in which bases such as DIEA, NMM, or non-hindered pyridine bases inhibit this step. These results led to the development of a stepwise procedure for peptide assembly in which 2,4,6-collidine is added to enhance activation and subsequently DIEA is added to enhance coupling.

      Drug Discovery Today (2003), 8 (24), 1128-1137CODEN: DDTOFS; ISSN:1359-6446. (Elsevier Science Ltd.)
      A review. Click chem. is a modular approach that uses only the most practical and reliable chem. transformations. Its applications are increasingly found in all aspects of drug discovery, ranging from lead finding through combinatorial chem. and target-templated in situ chem., to proteomics and DNA research, using bioconjugation reactions. The copper-(I)-catalyzed 1,2,3-triazole formation from azides and terminal acetylenes is a particularly powerful linking reaction, due to its high degree of dependability, complete specificity, and the bio-compatibility of the reactants. The triazole products are more than just passive linkers; they readily assoc. with biol. targets, through hydrogen bonding and dipole interactions. Click chem., a modular approach based on highly reliable chem. transformations, is being applied in all aspects of drug discovery, ranging from lead finding to proteomics and DNA research.

      Tumor targeting of the IL-15 superagonist RLI by an anti-GD2 antibody strongly enhances its antitumor potency
      Vincent, Marie; Bessard, Anne; Cochonneau, Denis; Teppaz, Geraldine; Sole, Veronique; Maillasson, Mike; Birkle, Stephane; Garrigue-Antar, Laure; Quemener, Agnes; Jacques, Yannick
      International Journal of Cancer (2013), 133 (3), 757-765CODEN: IJCNAW; ISSN:0020-7136. (John Wiley & Sons, Inc.)
      Immunocytokines (ICKs) targeting cytokines to the tumor environment using antibodies directed against a tumor-assocd. antigen often have a higher therapeutic index than the corresponding unconjugated cytokines. Various ICKs displaying significant antitumoral effects in several murine tumor models have already been developed, and some of them, in particular interleukin (IL)-2-based ICKs, are in Phase II clin. trials. Although sharing common biol. activities with IL-2 in vitro, IL-15 is now considered as having a better potential in antitumor immunotherapeutical strategies and has been shown to be less toxic than IL-2 in preclin. studies. We previously developed the fusion protein RLI, linking a sol. form of human IL-15Rα-sushi+ domain to human IL-15. RLI showed better biol. activities than IL-15 in vitro as well as higher antitumoral effects in vivo in murine and human cancer models. Here, we investigated, in the context of an ICK, the effect of assocg. RLI with an antibody targeting the GD2 ganglioside, a validated tumoral target expressed on many neurectodermal tumors. Anti-GD2-RLI fully retained the cytokine potential of RLI and the antibody effector functions (antibody-dependent cellular cytotoxicity and complement-dependent cytotoxicity). It displayed strong antitumor activities in two syngeneic cancer models in immunocompetent mice (s.c. EL4 and metastatic NXS2). Its therapeutic potency was higher than those of RLI and anti-GD2 alone or in combination. We suggest that this is related to its bifunctional (cytokine and antibody) nature.

      A review. Interleukin (IL)-15 is a dangerous inflammatory cytokine that induces tumor necrosis factor-α, IL-1β, and inflammatory chemokines.       LigScore: a novel scoring function for predicting binding affinities
      Krammer, Andre; Kirchhoff, Paul D.; Jiang, X.; Venkatachalam, C. M.; Waldman, Marvin
      Journal of Molecular Graphics & Modelling (2005), 23 (5), 395-407CODEN: JMGMFI; ISSN:1093-3263. (Elsevier Inc.)
      We present two new empirical scoring functions, LigScore1 and LigScore2, that attempt to accurately predict the binding affinity between ligand mols. and their protein receptors. The LigScore functions consist of three distinct terms that describe the van der Waals interaction, the polar attraction between the ligand and protein, and the desolvation penalty attributed to the binding of the polar ligand atoms to the protein and vice versa. Utilizing a regression approach on a data set of 118 protein-ligand complexes we have obtained a linear equation, LigScore2, using these three descriptors. LigScore2 has good predictability with regard to exptl. pKi values yielding a correlation coeff., r2, of 0.75 and a std. deviation of 1.04 over the training data set, which consists of a diverse set of proteins that span more than seven protein families.

Molecular recognition of the inhibitor AG-1343 by HIV-1 protease: conformationally flexible docking by evolutionary programming Chem. Biol. 1995, 2, 317– 324 DOI: 10.1016/1074-5521(95)90050-0
      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
      47
      Molecular recognition of the inhibitor AG-1343 by HIV-1 protease: Conformationally flexible docking by evolutionary programming
      Gehlhaar, Daniel K.; Verkhivker, Gennady M.; Rejto, Paul A.; Sherman, Christopher J.; Fogel, David B.; Fogel, Lawrence J.; Freer, Stephan T.
      Chemistry & Biology (1995), 2 (5), 317-24CODEN: CBOLE2; ISSN:1074-5521. (Current Biology)
      An important prerequisite for computational structure-based drug design is prediction of the structures of ligand-protein complexes that have not yet been exptl. detd. by x-ray crystallog. or NMR. For this task, docking of rigid ligands is inadequate because it assumes knowledge of the conformation of the bound ligand. Docking of flexible ligands would be desirable, but requires one to search an enormous conformational space. We set out to develop a strategy for flexible docking by combining a simple model of ligand-protein interactions for mol. recognition with an evolutionary programming search technique. We have developed an intermol. energy function that incorporates steric and hydrogen-bonding terms. The parameters in this function were obtained by docking in three different protein systems. The effectiveness of this method was demonstrated by conformationally flexible docking of the inhibitor AG-1343, a potential new drug against AIDS, into HIV-1 protease. For this mol., which has nine rotatable bonds, the crystal structure was reproduced within 1.5 Å rootmean-square deviation 34 times in 100 simulations, each requiring eight minutes on a Silicon Graphics R4400 workstation. The energy function correctly evaluates the crystal structure as the global energy min. We believe that a soln. of the docking problem may be achieved by matching a simple model of mol. recognition with an efficient search procedure. The necessary ingredients of a mol. recognition model include only steric and hydrogen-bond interaction terms. Although these terms are not necessarily sufficient to predict binding affinity, they describe ligand-protein interactions faithfully enough to enable a docking program to predict the structure of the bound ligand. This docking strategy thus provides an important tool for the interdisciplinary field of rational drug design.

      A General and Fast Scoring Function for Protein-Ligand Interactions: A Simplified Potential Approach
      Muegge, Ingo; Martin, Yvonne C.
      Journal of Medicinal Chemistry (1999), 42 (5), 791-804CODEN: JMCMAR; ISSN:0022-2623. (American Chemical Society)
      A fast, simplified potential-based approach is presented that ests. the protein-ligand binding affinity based on the given 3D structure of a protein-ligand complex. This general, knowledge-based approach exploits structural information of known protein-ligand complexes extd. from the Brookhaven Protein Data Bank and converts it into distance-dependent Helmholtz free interaction energies of protein-ligand atom pairs (potentials of mean force, PMF). The definition of an appropriate ref. state and the introduction of a correction term accounting for the vol. taken by the ligand were found to be crucial for deriving the relevant interaction potentials that treat solvation and entropic contributions implicitly. A significant correlation between exptl. binding affinities and computed score was found for sets of diverse protein-ligand complexes and for sets of different ligands bound to the same target. For 77 protein-ligand complexes taken from the Brookhaven Protein Data Bank, the calcd. score showed a std. deviation from obsd. binding affinities of 1.8 log Ki units and an R2 value of 0.61. The best results were obtained for the subset of 16 serine protease complexes with a std. deviation of 1.0 log Ki unit and an R2 value of 0.86. A set of 33 inhibitors modeled into a crystal structure of HIV-1 protease yielded a std. deviation of 0.8 log Ki units from measured inhibition consts. and an R2 value of 0.74. In contrast to empirical scoring functions that show similar or sometimes better correlation with obsd. binding affinities, our method does not involve deriving specific parameters that fit the obsd. binding affinities of protein-ligand complexes of a given training set. We compared the performance of the PMF score, B.ovrddot.ohm's score (LUDI), and the SMOG score for eight different test sets of protein-ligand complexes. It was found that for the majority of test sets the PMF score performs best. The strength of the new approach presented here lies in its generality as no knowledge about measured binding affinities is needed to derive at. interaction potentials. The use of the new scoring function in docking studies is outlined.

      [Crossref], [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
      49
      LigandFit: a novel method for the shape-directed rapid docking of ligands to protein active sites
      Venkatachalam, C. M.; Jiang, X.; Oldfield, T.; Waldman, M.
      Journal of Molecular Graphics & Modelling (2003), 21 (4), 289-307CODEN: JMGMFI; ISSN:1093-3263. (Elsevier Science Inc.)
      We present a new shape-based method, LigandFit, for accurately docking ligands into protein active sites. The method employs a cavity detection algorithm for detecting invaginations in the protein as candidate active site regions. A shape comparison filter is combined with a Monte Carlo conformational search for generating ligand poses consistent with the active site shape. Candidate poses are minimized in the context of the active site using a grid-based method for evaluating protein-ligand interaction energies. Errors arising from grid interpolation are dramatically reduced using a new non-linear interpolation scheme. Results are presented for 19 diverse protein-ligand complexes. The method appears quite promising, reproducing the x-ray structure ligand pose within an RMS of 2 Å in 14 out of the 19 complexes. A high-throughput screening study applied to the thymidine kinase receptor is also presented in which LigandFit, when combined with LigScore, an internally developed scoring function, yields very good hit rates for a ligand pool seeded with known actives.

      FAF-Drugs3: a web server for compound property calculation and chemical library design
      Lagorce, David; Sperandio, Olivier; Baell, Jonathan B.; Miteva, Maria A.; Villoutreix, Bruno O.
      Nucleic Acids Research (2015), 43 (W1), W200-W207CODEN: NARHAD; ISSN:0305-1048. (Oxford University Press)
      A review. Drug attrition late in preclin. or clin. development is a serious economic problem in the field of drug discovery. These problems can be linked, in part, to the quality of the compd. collections used during the hit generation stage and to the selection of compds. undergoing optimization. Here, we present FAF-Drugs3, a web server that can be used for drug discovery and chem. biol. projects to help in prepg. compd. libraries and to assist decision-making during the hit selection/lead optimization phase. Since it was first described in 2006, FAF-Drugs has been significantly modified. The tool now applies an enhanced structure curation procedure, can filter or analyze mols. with user-defined or eight predefined physicochem. filters as well as with several simple ADMET (absorption, distribution, metab., excretion and toxicity) rules. In addn., compds. can be filtered using an updated list of 154 hand-curated structural alerts while Pan Assay Interference compds. (PAINS) and other, generally unwanted groups are also investigated. FAF-Drugs3 offers access to user-friendly html result pages and the possibility to download all computed data. The server requires as input an SDF file of the compds.; it is open to all users and can be accessed without registration.

        Kirman, I.; Nielsen, O. H. Increased numbers of interleukin-15-expressing cells in active ulcerative colitis Am. J. Gastroenterol. 1996, 91, 1789– 1794
        [PubMed], [CAS], Google Scholar
        3
        Increased numbers of interleukin-15-expressing cells in active ulcerative colitis
        Kirman I; Nielsen O H
        The American journal of gastroenterology (1996), 91 (9), 1789-94 ISSN:0002-9270.
        OBJECTIVES: Interleukin-15 (IL-15) is a novel cytokine sharing many of the activities of IL-2. The goal of this study was to evaluate intracellular and serum IL-15 in ulcerative colitis (UC) and Crohn's disease (CD). METHODS: Intracellular expression of IL-15 in peripheral blood mononuclear cells (PBMC) from UC patients, CD patients, and controls was studied using cell permeabilization and staining with monoclonal antibodies. Serum levels of IL-15 were detected using ELISA. RESULTS: Percentage of IL-15 expressing PBMC was increased in UC patients and in five of six of CD patients with moderate and severe disease activity compared with controls. The number of IL-15 expressing cells in patients with active inflammatory bowel disease (IBD) declined within 2 wk of treatment. Serum IL-15 reached detectable levels in 62.5% of UC patients with moderate and severe disease activity but not in UC patients with slight disease activity or in remission, neither in CD patients nor in controls. In vitro lipopolysaccharide (LPS)-induced activation of PBMC from controls was associated with up-regulation of intracellular IL-15 expression (p < 0.01) and release of IL-15. CONCLUSIONS: UC patients with moderate and severe disease activity have increased percentage of IL-15 expressing PBMC, which might be induced by in vivo cell activation and can lead to elevation of released IL-15 in serum. Increased IL-15 expression after in vitro LPS stimulation of control PBMC suggests a nonspecific production of this cytokine during the immunoinflammatory response.
